				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Family of cell surface receptors</div>
<p><b>Pattern recognition receptors</b> (<b>PRRs</b>)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> play a crucial role in the proper function of the <a href="/wiki/Innate_immune_system" title="Innate immune system">innate immune system</a>. PRRs are germline-encoded host sensors, which detect molecules typical for the pathogens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kumar&#95;2011&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar_2011-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They are <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> expressed mainly by cells of the innate immune system, such as dendritic cells, macrophages, monocytes, neutrophils, as well as by epithelial cells,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to identify two classes of molecules: <a href="/wiki/Pathogen-associated_molecular_pattern" title="Pathogen-associated molecular pattern">pathogen-associated molecular patterns</a> (PAMPs), which are associated with microbial <a href="/wiki/Pathogens" class="mw-redirect" title="Pathogens">pathogens</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Damage-associated_molecular_pattern" title="Damage-associated molecular pattern">damage-associated molecular patterns</a> (DAMPs), which are associated with components of host's cells that are released during cell damage or death. They are also called <b>primitive pattern recognition receptors</b> because they evolved before other parts of the immune system, particularly before <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immunity" class="mw-redirect" title="Adaptive immunity">adaptive immunity</a>. PRRs also mediate the initiation of antigen-specific adaptive immune response and release of inflammatory cytokines.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kumar&#95;2011&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar_2011-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Takeda&#95;2003&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Takeda_2003-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> PRRs are regulated through a variety of pathways ensure optimal immune and inflammatory response to invaders.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:02&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The microbe-specific molecules that are recognized by a given PRR are called pathogen-associated molecular patterns (PAMPs) and include bacterial carbohydrates (such as <a href="/wiki/Lipopolysaccharide" title="Lipopolysaccharide">lipopolysaccharide</a> or LPS, <a href="/wiki/Mannose" title="Mannose">mannose</a>), nucleic acids (such as bacterial or viral DNA or RNA), bacterial peptides (flagellin, microtubule elongation factors), <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">peptidoglycans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipoteichoic_acid" title="Lipoteichoic acid">lipoteichoic acids</a> (from Gram-positive bacteria), <a href="/wiki/N-Formylmethionine" title="N-Formylmethionine"><i>N</i>-formylmethionine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipoprotein" title="Lipoprotein">lipoproteins</a> and fungal <a href="/wiki/Glucan" title="Glucan">glucans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>. PRRs exhibit significant diversity in coevolution with PAMPs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Endogenous stress signals are called damage-associated molecular patterns (DAMPs) and include <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a> and extracellular <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>, among many other compounds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kumar&#95;2011&#95;2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar_2011-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are several subgroups of PRRs. They are classified according to their <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> specificity, function, localization and/or evolutionary relationships.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Types_and_signaling">Types and signaling</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Types and signaling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Based on their localization, PRRs may be divided into membrane-bound PRRs and cytoplasmic PRRs:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Membrane-bound PRRs</i> include <a href="/wiki/Toll-like_receptor" title="Toll-like receptor">toll-like receptors (TLRs)</a> and <a href="/wiki/C-type_lectin_receptor" class="mw-redirect" title="C-type lectin receptor">C-type lectin receptors (CLRs)</a>.</li>
<li><i>Cytoplasmic PRRs</i> include <a href="/wiki/NOD-like_receptor" title="NOD-like receptor">NOD-like receptors (NLRs)</a> and <a href="/wiki/RIG-I-like_receptor" title="RIG-I-like receptor">RIG-I-like receptors (RLRs)</a>.</li></ul>
<p>PRRs were first discovered in plants.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid8525370&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8525370-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since that time many plant PRRs have been predicted by genomic analysis (370 in rice; 47 in <i>Arabidopsis</i>). Unlike animal PRRs, which are associated with intracellular kinases via adaptor proteins (see non-RD kinases below), plant PRRs are composed of an extracellular domain, transmembrane domain, juxtamembrane domain and intracellular kinase domain as part of a single protein.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Toll-like_receptors_(TLR)"><span id="Toll-like_receptors_.28TLR.29"></span>Toll-like receptors (TLR)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Toll-like receptors (TLR)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Recognition of extracellular or endosomal pathogen-associated molecular patterns is mediated by transmembrane proteins known as <a href="/wiki/Toll-like_receptor" title="Toll-like receptor">toll-like receptors</a> (TLRs).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid16551253&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16551253-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> TLRs share a typical structural motif, the <a href="/wiki/Leucine_rich_repeat" class="mw-redirect" title="Leucine rich repeat">leucine rich repeats (LRR)</a>, which give them their specific appearance and are also responsible for TLR functionality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Toll-like receptors were first discovered in <i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">Drosophila</a></i> and trigger the synthesis and secretion of <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> and activation of other host defense programs that are necessary for both innate or adaptive immune responses. 10 functional members of the TLR family have been described in humans so far.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Takeda&#95;2003&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Takeda_2003-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies have been conducted on <a href="/wiki/TLR11" class="mw-redirect" title="TLR11">TLR11</a> as well, and it has been shown that it recognizes flagellin and profilin-like proteins in mice.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nonetheless, TLR11 is only a <a href="/wiki/Pseudogene" title="Pseudogene">pseudogene</a> in humans without direct function or functional protein expression. Each of the TLR has been shown to interact with a specific PAMP.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Takeda&#95;2003&#95;5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Takeda_2003-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ozinsky&#95;2000&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ozinsky_2000-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="TLR_signaling">TLR signaling</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: TLR signaling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>TLRs tend to dimerize, <a href="/wiki/TLR4" class="mw-redirect" title="TLR4">TLR4</a> forms <a href="/wiki/Homodimers" class="mw-redirect" title="Homodimers">homodimers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/TLR6" class="mw-redirect" title="TLR6">TLR6</a> can dimerize with either <a href="/wiki/TLR_1" class="mw-redirect" title="TLR 1">TLR1</a> or <a href="/wiki/TLR2" class="mw-redirect" title="TLR2">TLR2</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ozinsky&#95;2000&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ozinsky_2000-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Interaction of TLRs with their specific PAMP is mediated through either <a href="/wiki/MYD88" title="MYD88">MyD88</a>-dependent pathway and triggers the signaling through <a href="/wiki/NF-%CE%BAB" title="NF-╬║B">NF-╬║B</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mitogen-activated_protein_kinase" title="Mitogen-activated protein kinase">MAP kinase</a> pathway and therefore the secretion of pro-inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> and co-stimulatory molecules or <a href="/wiki/TRIF" class="mw-redirect" title="TRIF">TRIF</a> ŌĆō dependent signaling pathway.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kumar&#95;2011&#95;2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar_2011-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Takeda&#95;2003&#95;5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Takeda_2003-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ozinsky&#95;2000&#95;12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ozinsky_2000-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> MyD88 ŌĆō dependent pathway is induced by various PAMPs stimulating the TLRs on macrophages and dendritic cells. MyD88 attracts the <a href="/wiki/IRAK4" title="IRAK4">IRAK4</a> molecule, IRAK4 recruits IRAK1 and IRAK2 to form a signaling complex. The signaling complex reacts with TRAF6 which leads to TAK1 activation and consequently the induction of inflammatory cytokines. The TRIF-dependent pathway is induced by macrophages and DCs after <a href="/wiki/TLR3" class="mw-redirect" title="TLR3">TLR3</a> and TLR4 stimulation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kumar&#95;2011&#95;2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar_2011-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Molecules released following TLR activation signal to other cells of the immune system making TLRs key elements of <a href="/wiki/Innate_immunity" class="mw-redirect" title="Innate immunity">innate immunity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immunity" class="mw-redirect" title="Adaptive immunity">adaptive immunity</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kumar&#95;2011&#95;2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar_2011-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid16930560&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16930560-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="C-type_lectin_receptors_(CLR)"><span id="C-type_lectin_receptors_.28CLR.29"></span>C-type lectin receptors (CLR)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: C-type lectin receptors (CLR)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many different cells of the innate immune system express a myriad of CLRs which shape innate immunity by virtue of their pattern recognition ability.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geijtenbeek&#95;2009&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geijtenbeek_2009-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Even though, most classes of human pathogens are covered by CLRs, CLRs are a major receptor for recognition of fungi:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hoving&#95;2014&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoving_2014-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> nonetheless, other PAMPs have been identified in studies as targets of CLRs  as well e.g. mannose is the recognition motif for many viruses, fungi and mycobacteria; similarly fucose presents the same for certain bacteria and helminths; and glucans are present on mycobacteria and fungi. In addition, many of acquired nonself surfaces e.g. carcinoembryonic/oncofetal type neoantigens carrying "internal danger source"/"self turned nonself" type pathogen pattern are also identified and destroyed (e.g. by complement fixation or other cytotoxic attacks) or sequestered (phagocytosed or ensheathed) by the immune system by virtue of the CLRs. The name lectin is a bit misleading because the family includes proteins with at least one C-type lectin domain (CTLD) which is a specific type of carbohydrate recognition domain. CTLD is a ligand binding motif found in more than 1000 known proteins (more than 100 in humans) and the ligands are often not sugars.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If and when the ligand is sugar they need <a href="/wiki/Calcium_in_biology" title="Calcium in biology">Ca2+</a> ŌĆō hence the name "C-type", but many of them do not even have a known sugar ligand thus despite carrying a lectin type fold structure, some of them are technically not "lectin" in function.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="CLR_signaling">CLR signaling</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: CLR signaling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There are several types of signaling involved in CLRs induced immune response, major connection has been identified between TLR and CLR signaling, therefore we differentiate between TLR-dependent and TLR-independent signaling. DC-SIGN leading to RAF1-MEK-ERK cascade, BDCA2 signaling via ITAM and signaling through ITIM belong among the TLR-dependent signaling. TLR-independent signaling such as Dectin 1, and Dectin 2 ŌĆō mincle signaling lead to <a href="/wiki/MAP_kinase" class="mw-redirect" title="MAP kinase">MAP kinase</a> and <a href="/wiki/NFkB" class="mw-redirect" title="NFkB">NFkB</a> activation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geijtenbeek&#95;2009&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geijtenbeek_2009-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hoving&#95;2014&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoving_2014-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Membrane receptor CLRs have been divided into 17 groups based on structure and phylogenetic origin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Generally there is a large group, which recognizes and binds carbohydrates, so called carbohydrate recognition domains (CRDs) and the previously mentioned CTLDs.
</p><p>Another potential characterization of the CLRs can be into mannose receptors and asialoglycoprotein receptors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geijtenbeek&#95;2009&#95;16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geijtenbeek_2009-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Group_I_CLRs:_The_mannose_receptors">Group I CLRs: The mannose receptors</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Group I CLRs: The mannose receptors"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mannose_receptor" title="Mannose receptor">mannose receptor</a> (MR)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is a PRR primarily present on the surface of <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cell" title="Dendritic cell">dendritic cells</a>. It belongs into the calcium-dependent multiple CRD group.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hoving&#95;2014&#95;17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoving_2014-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The MR belongs to the multilectin receptor protein group and, like the TLRs, provides a link between innate and adaptive immunity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid11899091&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11899091-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It recognizes and binds to repeated mannose units on the surfaces of infectious agents and its activation triggers endocytosis and phagocytosis of the microbe via the complement system. Specifically, mannose binding triggers recruitment of MBL-associated serine proteases (MASPs). The serine proteases activate themselves in a cascade, amplifying the immune response: MBL interacts with C4, binding the C4b subunit and releasing C4a into the bloodstream; similarly, binding of C2 causes release of C2b. Together, MBL, C4b and C2a are known as the C3 convertase. C3 is cleaved into its a and b subunits, and C3b binds the convertase. These together are called the C5 convertase. Similarly again, C5b is bound and C5a is released. C5b recruits C6, C7, C8 and multiple C9s. C5, C6, C7, C8 and C9 form the <a href="/wiki/Membrane_attack_complex" class="mw-redirect" title="Membrane attack complex">membrane attack complex</a> (MAC).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Group_II_CLRs:_asialoglycoprotein_receptor_family">Group II CLRs: asialoglycoprotein receptor family</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Group II CLRs: asialoglycoprotein receptor family"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>This is another large superfamily of CLRs that includes the classic <a href="/wiki/Asialoglycoprotein" title="Asialoglycoprotein">asialoglycoprotein</a> receptor <a href="/wiki/Macrophage_galactose-type_lectin_(MGL)" class="mw-redirect" title="Macrophage galactose-type lectin (MGL)">macrophage galactose-type lectin (MGL)</a>, <a href="/wiki/DC-SIGN" title="DC-SIGN">DC-SIGN</a> (CLEC4L), <a href="/wiki/Langerin" title="Langerin">Langerin</a> (CLEC4K), <a href="/w/index.php?title=Myeloid_DAP12%E2%80%91associating_lectin_(MDL)%E2%80%911&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Myeloid DAP12ŌĆæassociating lectin (MDL)ŌĆæ1 (page does not exist)">Myeloid DAP12ŌĆæassociating lectin (MDL)ŌĆæ1</a> (<a href="/wiki/CLEC5A" title="CLEC5A">CLEC5A</a>), DCŌĆæassociated CŌĆætype lectin 1 (Dectin1) subfamily, and DC immunoreceptor (<a href="/wiki/DCIR" class="mw-redirect" title="DCIR">DCIR</a>) subfamily. Furthermore, Dectin subfamily and DCIR subfamily consist of some members as follow. DCŌĆæassociated CŌĆætype lectin 1 (Dectin1) subfamily includes <a href="/wiki/Dectin_1" class="mw-redirect" title="Dectin 1">dectin 1</a>/<a href="/wiki/CLEC7A" title="CLEC7A">CLEC7A</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=DNGR1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="DNGR1 (page does not exist)">DNGR1</a>/<a href="/wiki/CLEC9A" title="CLEC9A">CLEC9A</a>, Myeloid CŌĆætype lectinŌĆælike receptor (MICL) (<a href="/wiki/CLEC12A" title="CLEC12A">CLEC12A</a>), CLEC2 (also called CLEC1B)- the platelet activation receptor for <a href="/wiki/Podoplanin" class="mw-redirect" title="Podoplanin">podoplanin</a> on lymphatic endothelial cells and invading front of some carcinomas, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=CLEC12B&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="CLEC12B (page does not exist)">CLEC12B</a>; while DC immunoreceptor (DCIR) subfamily includes DCIR/<a href="/wiki/CLEC4A" title="CLEC4A">CLEC4A</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dectin_2&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dectin 2 (page does not exist)">Dectin 2</a>/<a href="/wiki/CLEC6A" title="CLEC6A">CLEC6A</a>, Blood DC antigen 2 (BDCA2) ( <a href="/wiki/CLEC4C" title="CLEC4C">CLEC4C</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Mincle" class="mw-redirect" title="Mincle">Mincle</a> i.e. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Macrophage%E2%80%91inducible_C%E2%80%91type_lectin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="MacrophageŌĆæinducible CŌĆætype lectin (page does not exist)">macrophageŌĆæinducible CŌĆætype lectin</a> (<a href="/wiki/CLEC4E" class="mw-redirect" title="CLEC4E">CLEC4E</a>).
</p><p>The nomenclature (mannose versus asialoglycoprotein) is a bit misleading as these the asialoglycoprotein receptors are not necessarily <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a> (one of the commonest outer residues of asialo-glycoprotein) specific receptors and even many of this family members can also bind to <a href="/wiki/Mannose" title="Mannose">mannose</a> after which the other group is named.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="NOD-like_receptors_(NLR)"><span id="NOD-like_receptors_.28NLR.29"></span>NOD-like receptors (NLR)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: NOD-like receptors (NLR)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For more details, see <a href="/wiki/NOD-like_receptor" title="NOD-like receptor">NOD-like receptor</a>.</div>
<p>The NOD-like receptors (NLRs) are cytoplasmic proteins, which recognize bacterial peptidoglycans and mount proinflammatory and antimicrobial immune response.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Caruso&#95;2014&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caruso_2014-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Approximately 20 of these proteins have been found in the mammalian genome and include nucleotide-binding oligomerization domain (NODs), which binds <a href="/wiki/Nucleoside_triphosphate" title="Nucleoside triphosphate">nucleoside triphosphate</a>. Among other proteins the most important are: the <a href="/wiki/MHC_Class_II" class="mw-redirect" title="MHC Class II">MHC Class II</a> transactivator (<a href="/wiki/CIITA" title="CIITA">CIITA</a>), IPAF, BIRC1 etc.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid15870018&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15870018-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The ligands are currently known for <a href="/wiki/NOD1" title="NOD1">NOD1</a> and <a href="/wiki/NOD2" title="NOD2">NOD2</a>. NOD1 recognizes a molecule called meso-DAP, which is a <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">peptidoglycan</a> constituent only of <a href="/wiki/Gram_negative" class="mw-redirect" title="Gram negative">Gram negative</a> bacteria. NOD2 proteins recognize intracellular MDP (muramyl dipeptide), which is a peptidoglycan constituent of both Gram positive and Gram negative bacteria. When inactive, NODs are in the cytosol in a monomeric state and they undergo conformational change only after ligand recognition, which leads to their activation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Caruso&#95;2014&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caruso_2014-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> NODs transduce signals in the pathway of <a href="/wiki/NF-%CE%BAB" title="NF-╬║B">NF-╬║B</a> and <a href="/wiki/MAP_kinase" class="mw-redirect" title="MAP kinase">MAP kinases</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Serine-threonine_kinase" class="mw-redirect" title="Serine-threonine kinase">serine-threonine kinase</a> called RIP2. NODs signal via N-terminal <a href="/wiki/CARD_domain" class="mw-redirect" title="CARD domain">CARD domains</a> to activate downstream gene induction events, and interact with microbial molecules by means of a <a href="/wiki/C-terminal" class="mw-redirect" title="C-terminal">C-terminal</a> leucine-rich repeat (LRR) region.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid16493424&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16493424-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The interaction and cooperation among different types of receptors typical for the innate immune system has been established. An interesting cooperation has been discovered between TLRs and NLRs, particularly between TLR4 and NOD1 in response to <i>Escherichia coli</i> infection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another proof of the cooperation and integration of the entire immune system has been shown in vivo, when TLR signaling was inhibited or disabled, NOD receptors took over role of TLRs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Like NODs, NLRPs contain C-terminal LRRs, which appear to act as a regulatory domain and may be involved in the recognition of microbial pathogens. Also like NODs, these proteins contain a nucleotide binding site (NBS) for nucleoside triphosphates. Interaction with other proteins (e.g. the adaptor molecule <a href="/wiki/PYCARD" title="PYCARD">ASC</a>) is mediated via N-terminal pyrin (PYD) domain. There are 14 members of this protein subfamily in humans (called NLRP1 to NLRP14). NLRP3 and NLRP4 are responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Inflammasome" title="Inflammasome">inflammasome</a> activation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ip&#95;ncomms7931&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ip_ncomms7931-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> NLRP3 can be activated and give rise to <a href="/wiki/NLRP3_inflammasome" class="mw-redirect" title="NLRP3 inflammasome">NLRP3 inflammasome</a> by ATP, bacterial pore-forming toxins, alum and crystals. Alongside the listed molecules, which lead to activation of NLRP3 inflammasome, the assembly and activation can also be induced by K<sup>+</sup> efflux, Ca<sup>2+</sup> influx, disruption of lysosomes and ROS originating from mitochondria.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ip&#95;ncomms7931&#95;29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ip_ncomms7931-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The NLRP3 inflammasome is essential for induction of effective immune response. The NLRP3 inflammasome can be induced by a wide range of stimuli in contrast to the NLRP4 inflammasome, which binds more limited number and variety of ligands and works in a complex with NAIP protein.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other NLRs such as IPAF and NAIP5/Birc1e have also been shown to activate caspase-1 in response to <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Legionella" title="Legionella">Legionella</a></i>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="NLR_signaling">NLR signaling</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: NLR signaling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some of these proteins recognize endogenous or microbial molecules or stress responses and form oligomers that, in animals, activate inflammatory caspases (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Caspase_1" title="Caspase 1">caspase 1</a>) causing cleavage and activation of important inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_1" class="mw-redirect" title="Interleukin 1">IL-1</a>, and/or activate the <a href="/wiki/NF-%CE%BAB" title="NF-╬║B">NF-╬║B</a> signaling pathway to induce production of inflammatory molecules.
</p><p>The NLR family is known under several different names, including the CATERPILLER (or CLR) or NOD-LRR family.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid15870018&#95;25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15870018-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid15952891&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15952891-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The most significant members of the NLRs are NOD1 and NOD2. They sense the conserved microbial peptidoglycans in the cytoplasm of the cell and therefore represent another level of immune response after membrane-bound receptors such as TLRs and CLRs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Caruso&#95;2014&#95;24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caruso_2014-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This family of proteins is greatly expanded in plants, and constitutes a core component of <a href="/wiki/Plant_disease_resistance" title="Plant disease resistance">plant immune systems</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="RIG-I-like_receptors_(RLR)"><span id="RIG-I-like_receptors_.28RLR.29"></span>RIG-I-like receptors (RLR)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: RIG-I-like receptors (RLR)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/RIG-I-like_receptor" title="RIG-I-like receptor">RIG-I-like receptor</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Simon_(RIG-I_and_Mda5).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/Simon_%28RIG-I_and_Mda5%29.jpg/250px-Simon_%28RIG-I_and_Mda5%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="365" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/Simon_%28RIG-I_and_Mda5%29.jpg/500px-Simon_%28RIG-I_and_Mda5%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="1050" /></a><figcaption>RIG-I and Mda5-mediated signalling pathway.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Three RLR helicases have so far been described: <a href="/wiki/RIG-I" title="RIG-I">RIG-I</a> and <a href="/wiki/MDA5" title="MDA5">MDA5</a> (recognizing 5'triphosphate-RNA and dsRNA, respectively), which activate antiviral signaling, and <a href="/wiki/LGP2" title="LGP2">LGP2</a>, which appears to act as a dominant-negative inhibitor. RLRs initiate the release of inflammatory cytokines and type I interferon (IFN I).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kumar&#95;2011&#95;2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar_2011-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="RLR_signaling">RLR signaling</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: RLR signaling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>RLRs are <a href="/wiki/Rna_helicases" class="mw-redirect" title="Rna helicases">RNA helicases</a>, which have been shown to participate in intracellular recognition of viral double-stranded (ds) and single stranded <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> which recruit factors via twin N-terminal <a href="/wiki/CARD_domain" class="mw-redirect" title="CARD domain">CARD domains</a> to activate antiviral gene programs, which may be exploited in therapy of viral infections.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has been suggested that the main antiviral program induced by RLR is based on <a href="/wiki/ATPase" title="ATPase">ATPase</a> activity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> RLRs often interact and create cross-talk with the TLRs in the innate immune response and in regulation of adaptive immune response.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Secreted_PRRs">Secreted PRRs</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Secreted PRRs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A number of PRRs do not remain associated with the cell that produces them. <a href="/wiki/Complement_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Complement (biology)">Complement receptors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collectin" title="Collectin">collectins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ficolin" title="Ficolin">ficolins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pentraxin" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentraxin">pentraxins</a> such as serum <a href="/wiki/Amyloid" title="Amyloid">amyloid</a> and <a href="/wiki/C-reactive_protein" title="C-reactive protein">C-reactive protein</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lipid_transferase&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lipid transferase (page does not exist)">lipid transferases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan_recognition_protein" title="Peptidoglycan recognition protein">peptidoglycan recognition proteins</a> (PGRPs)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the LRR, XA21D<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid9596635&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9596635-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> are all secreted proteins. One very important collectin is <a href="/wiki/Mannan-binding_lectin" title="Mannan-binding lectin">mannan-binding lectin</a> (MBL), a major PRR of the innate immune system that binds to a wide range of bacteria, viruses, fungi and protozoa.  MBL predominantly recognizes certain sugar groups on the surface of microorganisms but also binds <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a> and non-<a href="/wiki/Glycosylation" title="Glycosylation">glycosylated</a> proteins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid16948640&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16948640-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Once bound to the ligands MBL and Ficolin oligomers recruit <a href="/wiki/MASP1_(protein)" title="MASP1 (protein)">MASP1</a> and <a href="/wiki/MASP2_(protein)" title="MASP2 (protein)">MASP2</a> and initiate the <a href="/wiki/Mannan-binding_lectin_pathway" class="mw-redirect" title="Mannan-binding lectin pathway">lectin pathway</a> of complement activation which is somewhat similar to the <a href="/wiki/Classical_complement_pathway" title="Classical complement pathway">classical complement pathway</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_plants">In plants</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: In plants"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Plants contain a significant number of PRRs that share remarkable structural and functional similarity with <i>Drosophila</i> Toll and mammalian TLRs. The first PRR identified in plants or animals was the Xa21 protein, conferring resistance to the Gram-negative bacterial pathogen <i>Xanthomonas oryzae</i> pv. <i>oryzae</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid8525370&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8525370-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid24482761&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24482761-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since that time two other plants PRRs, <i>Arabidopsis</i> FLS2 (flagellin) and EFR (elongation factor Tu receptor) have been isolated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid19400727&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19400727-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More than 600 receptor-kinase genes and 57 receptor-like proteins have been reported in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome since 2019.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Noman&#95;1882&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noman_1882-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Plant PRRs either exist as surface-localized receptor kinases (RKs) or receptor-like proteins (RLPs) that contain multiple ligand-binding ectodomains that perceive PAMPs or DAMPs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The corresponding PAMPs for FLS2 and EFR have been identified.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid19400727&#95;41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19400727-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Upon ligand recognition, the plant PRRs transduce "PAMP-triggered immunity" (PTI).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid16497589&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16497589-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Plant_disease_resistance" title="Plant disease resistance">Plant immune systems</a> also encode resistance proteins that resemble NOD-like receptors (see above), that feature NBS and <a href="/wiki/LRR_domain" class="mw-redirect" title="LRR domain">LRR domains</a> and can also carry other conserved interaction domains such as the TIR cytoplasmic domain found in Toll and interleukin receptors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid16677430&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16677430-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The nucleotide-binding and leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) proteins are required for detecting nonindigenous molecular signatures from pathogens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Noman&#95;1882&#95;42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noman_1882-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Plant PRRs are associated with the innate immune system while NBS-LRR proteins are initiated in the adaptive immune system called the <a href="/wiki/Effector-triggered_immunity" title="Effector-triggered immunity">effector-triggered immunity</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Noman&#95;1882&#95;42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noman_1882-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="NonRD_kinases">NonRD kinases</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: NonRD kinases"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>PRRs commonly associate with or contain members of a monophyletic group of kinases called the interleukin-1 receptor-associated kinase (IRAK) family that include <i>Drosophila</i> Pelle, human IRAKs, rice XA21 and <i>Arabidopsis</i> FLS2. In mammals, PRRs can also associate with members of the receptor-interacting protein (RIP) kinase family, distant relatives to the IRAK family. Some IRAK and RIP family kinases fall into a small functional class of kinases termed non-RD, many of which do not autophosphorylate the activation loop. A survey of the yeast, fly, worm, human, Arabidopsis, and rice kinomes (3,723 kinases) revealed that despite the small number of non-RD kinases in these genomes (9ŌĆō29%), 12 of 15 kinases known or predicted to function in PRR signaling fall into the non-RD class. In plants, all PRRs characterized to date belong to the non-RD class. These data indicate that kinases associated with PRRs can largely be predicted by the lack of a single conserved residue and reveal new potential plant PRR subfamilies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid16424920&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16424920-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Regulation">Regulation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Regulation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>PRRs and associated elements require strict regulation to maximize pathogen defense and minimize self-damage. There are a variety of mechanisms currently known to modulate the regulation of PRRs. At the DNA level: <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">histone modification</a>, transcription factors, and RNA interference with <a href="/wiki/Chromatin_remodeling" title="Chromatin remodeling">chromatin</a> regulate the transcription of PRR genes. For example, TLRs in humans are primary response genes that switch transcriptional machinery to increasingly produce mature transcripts upon LPS exposure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After transcription, checkpoints during mRNA splicing and transport regulate the survival of miRNA. Notably in plants, <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">miRNA</a> and secondary <a href="/wiki/Small_interfering_RNA" title="Small interfering RNA">siRNA</a> production are used to regulate the production of NBR-LRRs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Post-translational regulation also plays an important role, with ubiquitylation, methylation, and phosphorylation emerging as modulators for PRR activity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:02&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In animals, it is well known that PRRs rely on phosphorylation of tyrosine rich domains, and expression of <a href="/wiki/Phosphatase" title="Phosphatase">phosphatases</a> can inhibit this crucial step in downstream signaling.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:02&#95;6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Evolution">Evolution</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Evolution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>PRRs and PAMPs are under constant <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">co-evolutionary</a> pressure: this can lead to an expansion in the diversity of PRRs. Prominent examples include the diversity of PRR inceptin receptor (INR)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and evolution of FLS3 from FLS2 receptors in plants,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as the C-type lectin (CTL) and Down syndrome cell adhesion molecule (Dscam) in insects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In animals, ongoing research seeks to understand polymorphisms present in Toll-like receptor 4 again the variation of the Lipid A portion of LPS expressed in gram-negative bacteria.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> PRR diversity can occur in a variety of ways, including <a href="/wiki/Gene_duplication" title="Gene duplication">gene duplication</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neofunctionalization" title="Neofunctionalization">neofunctionalization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insertion_(genetics)" title="Insertion (genetics)">gene insertion</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Alternative_splicing" title="Alternative splicing">alternative splicing</a>. PRRs may evolve to target new MAMP <a href="/wiki/Epitope" title="Epitope">epitope</a> or to recognize the same MAMP epitope that has been slightly changed due to mutations in the pathogen. PRR diversity has been shown to correspond with different species that vary in their exposure to pathogens, suggesting that expansion of PRR diversity can occur relatively quickly and are thus under strong evolutionary pressure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Clinical_significance">Clinical significance</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Clinical significance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Immunotherapy">Immunotherapy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Immunotherapy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Research groups have recently conducted extensive research into the involvement and potential use of patient's immune system in the therapy of various diseases, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Immunotherapy" title="Immunotherapy">immunotherapy</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Monoclonal_antibodies" class="mw-redirect" title="Monoclonal antibodies">monoclonal antibodies</a>, non-specific immunotherapies, <a href="/wiki/Oncolytic_virus" title="Oncolytic virus">oncolytic virus</a> therapy, <a href="/wiki/T-cell" class="mw-redirect" title="T-cell">T-cell</a> therapy and <a href="/wiki/Cancer_vaccines" class="mw-redirect" title="Cancer vaccines">cancer vaccines</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  NOD2 has been associated through a loss and gain of function with development of <a href="/wiki/Crohn%27s_disease" title="Crohn&#39;s disease">Crohn's disease</a> and early-onset <a href="/wiki/Sarcoidosis" title="Sarcoidosis">sarcoidosis</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Caruso&#95;2014&#95;24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caruso_2014-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mutations in <a href="/wiki/NOD2" title="NOD2">NOD2</a> in cooperation with environmental factors lead to development of chronic inflammation in the intestine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Caruso&#95;2014&#95;24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caruso_2014-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Therefore, it has been suggested to treat the disease by inhibiting the small molecules, which are able to modulate the NOD2 signaling, particularly RIP2. Two therapeutics have been approved by FDA so far inhibiting the <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylation" title="Phosphorylation">phosphorylation</a> on RIP2, which is necessary for proper NOD2 functioning, <a href="/wiki/Gefitinib" title="Gefitinib">gefitinib</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erlotinib" title="Erlotinib">erlotinib</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, research has been conducted on GSK583, a highly specific RIP2 inhibitor, which seems highly promising in inhibiting NOD1 and NOD2 signaling and therefore, limiting inflammation caused by NOD1, NOD2 signaling pathways.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another possibility is to remove the sensor for NOD2, which has been proved efficient in murine models in the effort to suppress the symptoms of Crohn's disease.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Type_II_kinase&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Type II kinase (page does not exist)">Type II kinase</a> inhibitors, which are highly specific, have shown promising results in blocking the TNF arising from NOD-dependent pathways, which shows a high potential in treatment of inflammation associated tumors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Infection_and_carcinogenesis">Infection and carcinogenesis</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Infection and carcinogenesis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Another possible exploitation of PRRs in human medicine is also related to tumor malignancies of the intestines. <i><a href="/wiki/Helicobacter_pylori" title="Helicobacter pylori">Helicobacter pylori</a></i> has been shown by studies to significantly correlate with the development of a gastrointestinal tumors. In a healthy individual <i>Helicobacter pylori</i> infection is targeted by the combination of PRRs, namely TLRs, NLRs, RLRs and CLR DC-SIGN. In case of their malfunction, these receptors have also been connected to carcinogenesis. When the <i>Helicobacter pylori</i> infection is left to progress in the intestine it develops into chronic inflammation, atrophy and eventually dysplasia leading to development of cancer. Since all types of PRRs play a role in the identification and eradication of the infection, their specific agonists mount a strong immune response to cancers and other PRR-related diseases. The inhibition of TLR2 has been shown to significantly correlate with improved state of the patient and suppression of the gastric adenocarcinoma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Neurodegenerative_and_metabolic_disease">Neurodegenerative and metabolic disease</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pattern_recognition_receptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Neurodegenerative and metabolic disease"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The PRRs are also tightly connected to the proper function of neuronal networks and tissues, especially because of their involvement in the processes of inflammation, which are essential for proper function but may cause irreparable damage if not under control. The TLRs are expressed on most cells of the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (CNS) and they play a crucial role in sterile inflammation. After an injury, they lead to impairment of axonal growth and slow down or even halt the recovery altogether. Another important structure involved in and potentially exploitable in therapy after injury is the <a href="/wiki/Inflammasome" title="Inflammasome">inflammasome</a>. Through its induction of proinflammatory cytokines, IL-1╬▓ and IL-18, it has been proposed that inhibition of inflammasome may also serve as an efficient therapeutic method.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kigerl&#95;2014&#95;5ŌĆō16&#95;62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kigerl_2014_5ŌĆō16-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The involvement of inflammasome has also been researched in several other diseases including <a href="/wiki/Experimental_autoimmune_encephalomyelitis" title="Experimental autoimmune encephalomyelitis">experimental autoimmune encephalomyelitis</a> (EAE), <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Alzheimer&#39;s">Alzheimer's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson&#39;s disease">Parkinson's diseases</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a> connected with <a href="/wiki/Type_II_diabetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Type II diabetes">type II diabetes</a> in patients. The suggested therapies include degradation of NLRP3 or inhibit the proinflammatory cytokines.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kigerl&#95;2014&#95;5ŌĆō16&#95;62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kigerl_2014_5ŌĆō16-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
